--- page 351 ---
•
Streetscape standards (page 73 of HSAP)
-
Street trees shall be planted along both streets in the Station Area at consistency intervals of 
20 to 30 feet. 
-
In order to complement El Camino Real and create a cohesive character for the Station Area, 
tree species should be the species recommended by the City’s Street Tree Master Plan. 
-
Trees should be planted in grates that are ADA compliant, such that the grates do not 
present any obstacle for persons with mobility impediments. 
-
These standards do not apply to streets internal to the Hillsdale Shopping Center. 
Landscaping improvements on these streets should be part of a landscape master plan for 
the Shopping Center. 
•
Street Furniture and Street Lights (page 73 of HSAP).
-
Street furniture and street lights shall be provided as described for the Station Area in A. 
Station Area Sidewalks. 
-
Pedestrian amenities must be strategically located to ensure ADA compliance and a clear 
path of travel for pedestrians. 
•
Station Area Sidewalks (Page 68 and 69 of HSAP):
-
Edge Zone The edge zone should create a clear end to the vehicle realm and transition to 
the pedestrian realm. 
-
Furnishing Zone The furnishing zone should be a minimum of 3 feet in width, including space 
for street trees, street furniture, and lights. To the extent possible, street furniture such as 
newspaper racks should be grouped to keep space clear for pedestrian travel. 
-
Throughway Zone The throughway zone should be a minimum of 5 feet in width and free of 
all obstructions to meet ADA requirements for pedestrian travel. Where sidewalks are wider 
than 8 feet total, it should be a minimum of 50% of the total sidewalk width, or 5 feet, 
whichever is greater. 
-
Frontage Zone The frontage zone should be used by businesses to engage passers-by and 
provide items of visual interest. The frontage zone should be a minimum of 2 feet in width. 
•
Hillsdale Shopping Center Development Guidelines (Page 80 and 81 of HSAP):
Pedestrian Bridge 
-
Consider modifications to the pedestrian/retail bridge over 31st Avenue to allow for better 
visual connections to the street below, and light to the street below, and for more light 
down to street level. 
-
Consider installation of enhanced crosswalk treatments, such as lighted cross-walks, to 
increase the visibility of pedestrians crossing the street. 
Buildings 
-
All new or remodeled buildings should be pedestrian-scale and designed to add visual 
interest to the street. 
-
Entries to retail uses should open onto the sidewalk where possible. 
-
In order to complement El Camino Real and create a cohesive character for the Station Area, 
buildings should be well-designed and follow the guidelines from the El Camino Real Master 
Plan. In general, blank ground floor building walls should be avoided while design elements 
that heighten visibility and transparency are strongly encouraged. 
-
New buildings are strongly encouraged to be constructed on existing surface parking areas 
that front the street in order to reduce the visual prominence of parking lots. 
351 of 551

--- page 352 ---
-
The bookends of the existing parking structure are encouraged to be redesigned as retail 
uses with at least a 40- foot depth. 
•
Policy TRA-1.1: Integrate and connect the Station Area street system with the surrounding City 
streets, including tying into the grid system established in Bay Meadows Phase II.
•
Policy TRA-1.2: Provide Station Area streets that support multiple modes of travel, while 
incorporating the functional typology and design consistent with maintaining adjacent uses.
•
Policy TRA-4.1: New development on properties in the Station Area must develop a Trip 
Reduction and Parking Management Program, following the recommendations in Appendix A of 
this Plan, including implementing the required and optional measures for both employers and 
residential developments.  The Hillsdale Shopping Center shall complete such a program, but 
may make implementation optional for employers that are tenants of the Shopping Center.
•
Policy TRA-4.2: Expand the Transportation Management Association (TMA) formed under the 
Rail Corridor Plan to include all properties within the Station Area and require that all new 
development join the TMA.  The Hillsdale Shopping Center’s participation in the TMA will consist 
of optional measures, but it will not be subject to the TMA’s trip reduction goals.
•
Policy TRA-5.1: Link the pedestrian and bicycle circulation system within the Station Area to the 
existing and planned city-wide and regional pedestrian and bicycle circulation facilities, including 
connecting to residential areas west of El Camino Real and south of Hillsdale Boulevard.  
•
Policy TRA-5.2: Create internal connections within the Station Area that maximize safe and 
appealing pedestrian-oriented circulation through the incorporation of design elements such as 
pedestrian-oriented lighting, wide landscaped sidewalks, curb extensions at intersections, and 
on-street parking to buffer pedestrians from vehicles.  Where feasible, as parcels are developed, 
add through-block pedestrian connections within the internal circulation of larger properties to 
create a finer-grained circulation pattern west of El Camino Real. 
•
Policy TRA-5.3: Promote increased east-west bicycle circulation through the Station Area by 
supporting the development of new bicycle facilities along 28th Avenue.  If feasible, support the 
development of new bicycle facilities on 31st Avenue, in conjunction with redevelopment of 
that portion of the Hillsdale Shopping Center.  The latter would only be considered feasible if a 
configuration can be developed that balances auto, bicycle, and pedestrian circulation on 31st 
Avenue.
•
Policy TRA-7.6: Conduct an evaluation of the City’s Building Security Code requirement for 
parking garages serving residential uses to have electrically operated closures separating 
resident and visitor parking. Examine best practices of other jurisdictions for alternative means 
that would allow for less restrictive access while still ensuring resident security.
•
Policy INF-1.1: Ensure that private development provides its fair share of funding for necessary 
improvements to infrastructure in the Station Area.
•
Policy INF-2.1: Ensure that water supply infrastructure and wastewater infrastructure are 
developed in advance of new development in the Station Area.
•
Policy INF-2.2: As downstream improvements are constructed with modifications to the railway, 
capacity should accommodate the potential redevelopment in the Station Area.  Facilities 
should not be sized for a lower capacity than the existing adjacent facilities.  Larger sized water 
lines could be placed in the potential roadway connections at the grade separations if the 
roadway connection projects are tied to the railway improvements.
•
Policy INF-3.1 Ensure that new development meets or exceeds storm drainage requirements, 
with a focus on providing green infrastructure solutions for stormwater management.
352 of 551

--- page 353 ---
•
Policy INF-3.2 Work with developers and the San Francisco Bay Regional Water Quality Control 
Board to encourage innovative treatment and detention methods, particularly methods that 
reduce the need for on-site mitigation.
•
Policy INF-4.1 Work with City of San Mateo Parks and Recreation as well as regional open space 
providers to ensure that Station Area residents have access to sufficient park areas, most of 
which are outside of the Station Area.  Access includes bicycle and pedestrian connections that 
are clear, as well as signage so that residents and visitors know where parks are located.
•
(Page 118) No subdivision map, use permit, design review application, or other entitlements will 
be issued for development projects in the Station Area until a finding has been made that the 
proposed project is consistent with this Station Area Plan.
•
(Page 122) Notwithstanding these findings, as redevelopment in the Station Area occurs, the

EL CAMINO REAL CORRIDOR PLAN
The El Camino Real Corridor Plan was adopted in 2001, and the following policies of Plan may be 
applicable to the project.
 
•
Gen 1.1.2: Side walls that do not front a side street may be without window or door openings, 
but should not be left "blank" if directly visible from El Camino. Visible side walls should be 
attractively finished with a considerate choice of materials and or color, or as a location for a 
special feature such as a mural. The facade treatment should be continued around the corner of 
the building for a minimum dimension of an expressed structural or architectural bay. 
•
Gen 1.1.3: In order to create a visually interesting environment and make interior building space 
visible to the street, clear glass display windows and entries containing glazing should comprise 
a minimum of 50% of the ground floor wall area. The use of reflective or dark tinted glass is 
discouraged, especially at the ground level. For the portion of the facade above the ground 
floor, glass curtain walls exceeding the width of the structural bays, horizontal ribbon windows 
and mirrored glass are discouraged. 
•
Gen 1.3.1: In order to provide valuable navigational information when moving along El Camino, 
at least one customer entry should be directly visible from the street. 
•
Gen 1.3.2: Buildings should have storefront entries directly accessible from El Camino Real. 
Buildings with the main entry on the side should include architectural elements that make the 
entry visible from El Camino and include a sidewalk from the street to the entry. Ground floor 
lighting and displays that effectively draw people into the interior space are strongly 
encouraged. Corner buildings, including those that front both a public sidewalk and a surface 
parking area, should have corner features which may include entries, architectural features, etc. 
(This guideline is especially important for buildings located at theme intersections.) Buildings set 
353 of 551

--- page 354 ---
back from the street edge should provide a pedestrian walkway from the sidewalk on El Camino 
Real to the entry frontage. 
•
Gen 1.5.3: Parapets should be provided to hide flat roofs and roof mounted equipment. The 
parapet can be designed, secondarily, as a decorative element or to provide a location for a sign 
as long as the design is compatible with the architecture of the entire building. 
•
Gen 2.1.1: In order to maintain a 10-foot effective sidewalk width along El Camino, buildings less 
than two stories should be set back from the El Camino property line sufficient to create 10 feet 
of effective sidewalk width inclusive of the existing width of the public sidewalk (measured from 
the back-of-curb to the property line). However, if the setback creates a hardship which can be 
proved by the applicant, the City may mitigate the hardship by reducing the width of the 
setback. 
•
Gen 2.1.2: Tree wells with ADA compatible metal grates should be consistently located within 
the effective sidewalk area adjacent to the buildings. Refer to the Streetscape Plan for 
recommended tree species within the effective sidewalk. 
•
Gen 2.1.3: At transit stops, pedestrian amenities as listed in the Streetscape Plan such as transit 
shelters, benches, lighting and trash receptacles should be provided. When such pedestrian 
amenities are included within the effective sidewalk width area, a minimum 5-foot wide path of 
travel measured from the back-of-curb shall be kept unobstructed for pedestrian movement. 
•
Gen 2.2.1: Buildings above two stories should be set back 10 feet from property line along El 
Camino Real. 
•
Gen 2.2.2: The 10 ft. effective sidewalk width consistent with Gen 2.1.1 - Gen 2.2.3 should be 
provided along the El Camino Real frontage. 
•
Gen 2.2.3: For buildings over two stories, the 10-foot setback from the property line extends 
beyond the minimum 10-foot effective sidewalk width. This required setback beyond the 10- 
foot effective sidewalk width should be landscaped in a manner consistent with the project and 
the landscape treatment adopted by the Streetscape Plan. Areas adjacent to building entries 
and display windows should be designed predominantly as a hardscape area for gathering and 
outdoor commercial activity with accent planters, raised beds, benches and/or other types of 
pedestrian amenities. 
•
Gen 2.4.1: Setbacks for surface parking lots and parking structures must create both an effective 
sidewalk width of 10 feet and a required 6 foot landscape setback (per Zoning Ordinance) 
behind the 10’ effective sidewalk. However, if the 6’ landscape setback creates a hardship 
(which can be proved by the applicant), the City may mitigate the hardship by reducing the 
width of the parking landscape setback. 
•
Gen 2.4.2: The 6-foot landscape setback for surface parking areas should be planted with 
species compatible with the Streetscape Plan to provide a strong visual edge along the street. A 
low wall at a height convenient for sitting, trellises, and low growing shrubs (max. 24 inches in 
height) can also provide a very effective way to screen the lower portion of parked cars from the 
354 of 551

--- page 355 ---
sidewalk. Access between surface parking areas and the public sidewalk should be provided at 
regular intervals along the parking frontage. 
•
Gen 2.5.1: A minimum of 50% of the total property line frontage on El Camino should be 
occupied by buildings located along the setback line. The building may only be set behind the 
setback line if the additional setback provides a public amenity such as a wider sidewalk, 
outdoor seating or outdoor dining, etc. If a building is to be set back beyond the recommended 
dimension, it is recommended that the setback not exceed 20 feet along El Camino, in order to 
create a comfortable pedestrian environment. It is recommended that building frontage at the 
setback line be contiguous, although the individual building facades may be articulated. 
•
Gen 2.5.2: For corner parcels, the building should continue at the side street setback line for a 
minimum of 50% of property frontage. In the case of shallow parcels, the 50% building frontage 
requirement may be reduced to accommodate necessary parking access from the side street. 
•
Gen 3.1.2: Buildings taller than two stories should step back above the second story to provide 
sculpting of the overall form as viewed from the street. 
•
Gen 3.2.3: Buildings should generally be oriented parallel to the El Camino right-of-way. The 
building facade may be angled, if it creates an opportunity for usable public space and/or 
enhances the building design. 
•
Gen 4.1.1: Surface parking area, including driveways, should not occupy more than 50% of a 
property frontage along El Camino. 
•
Gen 4.4.1: Open parking areas should have a 6’ landscape buffer along street frontages and 
adjacent residential areas. At least 10 percent of open parking areas should be landscaped with 
islands of minimum 5’ width be provided after every 10 parking spaces. Lots should have one 
tree planted for every three parking spaces, not including the street trees within the effective 
sidewalk. Planting areas should be protected from common vehicular traffic with a six-inch 
concrete curb. 
•
Gen 4.4.2: Tree species should be compatible with the list of recommended sidewalk tree trees 
in the Streetscape Plan. 
•
Gen 4.4.3: Ground level landscaping within surface parking areas should be less than 18-24 
inches to allow sight lines for vehicular and pedestrian safety. 
•
Gen 5.1.3: Multi-tenant buildings shall have a coordinated sign program. All signs within a single 
development shall have coordinated design placement and fabrication concept as part of a 
“Planned Signing District” and architectural review process. Individual tenant signs may be 
located on individual storefronts, over display windows and at entries and generally should be 
part of a master sign program for the entire building/complex. These sign elements may include 
projecting ("fin") signs, awning located signs, or, smaller, surface mounted signs. Painted and 
placard window signs are allowed such that they do not exceed a maximum of 25% coverage of 
the glazing and are compatible with the colors and materials of the building. Signs located within 
355 of 551

--- page 356 ---
windows should be located at the lower portion of the window to allow visibility into the 
businesses. 
•
Gen 5.2.1: Monument signs are the preferred sign type for businesses, which have driveway 
entrances along El Camino. The signs are encouraged to be low profile so they do not block sight 
lines for vehicles entering and exiting parking areas. The height of monument signs should be 
limited to 5 feet to allow pedestrians to see over the sign to the building. Monument signs 
should have architectural features consistent with the building and should not be internally 
illuminated. 
•
Gen 5.2.4: Signs can be located on the window glazing, but should be limited to 25% of the 
window area and should be compatible with the colors and materials of the building. Sign 
boards located in window display areas should be included in the 25% maximum. The preferred 
location for store hours is on the entry door as opposed to the display windows. 
•
Gen 5.2.5: The use of awnings for primary signage is discouraged. If a sign is to be located on an 
awning, it should be restricted to no more than 25% of the fabric area of the awning. Awning 
signs are considered more appropriate on the vertical portion, or “flap.” Signage on the sloped 
area is difficult to read, and should be limited to graphic images or logos as opposed to words. 
•
Gen 5.2.6: At present, the zoning ordinance (25.20-4) permits freestanding signs in C and M 
districts of a maximum size of 40 square feet on lots up to 50 feet in width to a height not to 
exceed 15 feet, with all signs over 8 feet requiring approval of a Site Plan and Architectural 
Review. Lots over 50 feet in width are permitted an additional sign area of 0.35 sq. ft/1 ft of 
frontage to a maximum size of 75 square feet and additional height of 0.1 ft/1 ft. of frontage to 
a maximum height of 25 feet. 
•
TI-4: Where buildings are on a corner lot, the 10-foot effective sidewalk setback (GEN-2.1.1) 
should continue around the corner to the rear property line of the side street frontage. 
However, if the setback creates a hardship (which can be proved by the applicant), the City may 
mitigate the hardship by reducing the width of the setback. Buildings at theme intersections are 
encouraged to provide a corner setback in addition to the effective sidewalk width. The setback 
area should be predominately hardscape with amenities such as attractive window displays, 
seating, shade and special lighting. 
•
ECR 4-1: Buildings within Hillsdale Shopping Center do not need to be located such that 50% of 
the building facades lie along the setback line. 
•
ECR 4-2: Well-designed surface parking lots and parking structures can occupy more than 50% of 
the setback frontage. 
•
ECR 4-3: Recognizing that Hillsdale is a regional shopping center that relies on automobile 
access, and has a landscaping buffer along El Camino Real, the 10-foot effective sidewalk width 
may be reduced. However, the ADA minimum of a 5’ unobstructed path of movement should be 
maintained at all times.

--- page 357 ---
ZONING ORDINANCE (MUNICIPAL CODE TITLE 27)
The following Code sections are related to the Hillsdale Station Area Plan.
•
SMMC Chapter 27.92 – Hillsdale Station Area Plan codifies development standards for Hillsdale 
Station Area Plan and outlines the relationships to other laws. Specifically, it states that “in the 
event of a conflict between the terms, conditions, requirements, or policies of the Hillsdale 
Station Area Plan and the San Mateo City Zoning Code, the Hillsdale Station Area Plan shall 
govern.” Zoning Ordinance identifies the permitted uses and special uses in Hillsdale Station 
Area Plan area shall follow the applicable zoning districts’ standards. 
However, the eastern half of the site along El Camino Real is designated as Mixed-Use High and 
the western half of the site is designated as Mixed-Use Medium II in General Plan 2040, while 
corresponding zoning district and development standards have not yet established. Therefore, 
the uses, densities, intensities and height specified in the General Plan shall control, pursuant to 
SMMC Section 27.02.220. 
•
SMMC Section 27.60.180(e) - Q5 Overlay District identifies the development standards and 
procedures for Hillsdale Mall area. Hillsdale main shopping site is identified as Sub-Area B and 
Sub-Area C. When 75% or more demolition or redevelopment of structures (including parking 
structures) occurs in any sub-areas, a comprehensive master plan for such sub-areas in 
consistent with the El Camino Real Master Plan and Hillsdale Station Area Plan is required. The 
development is required to meet the findings in Zoning Ordinance.

10/29/25, 11:42 AM Hillsdale Redevelopment -- Comments - Elaine Ling - Outlook

# Outlook

Hillsdale Redevelopment -- Comments

or

Date Tue 4/22/2025 3:04 PM
To Elaine Ling <eling@cityofsanmateo.org>

Hello Ms. Ling,

| am a resident of San Mateo Village and live very near to Hillsdale Mall. | had a few comments about
the proposed development | hope you'll consider:

1. Traffic Access
The proposed plan appears to show vehicle access from El Camino Real (ECR) to new roads on 32nd
and 33rd, as well as North South extensions of Sailer Drive and Edison Lane.

As I'm sure you're aware, traffic on Hillsdale, in the evening especially, can be brutal. | believe creating
more arteries to bypass the ECR/Hillsdale interchange would reduce the walkability proposed by the
project, increase noise for residents, and likely endanger pedestrians crossing those new
thoroughfares.

2. Road Improvements
The roads in and around Hillsdale Mall (largely ECR and Hillsdale) are shoddy and in need of serious

attention, including structural and drainage upgrades to protect the Hillsdale underpass from
flooding. | would also suggest moving the traffic light from Hillsdale/W. Sailer to Hillsdale and Edison
Street. That intersection gets busy and has a lot of pedestrian access.

3. Community Benefits

In addition to the park the development proposes, what other public benefit space would the
developers consider? The southern part of San Mateo is missing public indoor recreation areas. |
think a public pool could be a very nice amenity. | would also suggest that their park area be
expanded and perhaps a playground added for residents. What about a public meeting space
available for free rent by the Community? Provision that requires some sort of private daycare
Operator? Could the developer designate an area for a Farmer's Market or regular Food Trucks that
support local business? The latter two items would increase business for the Mall as well.

4. Laurel Creek

This is a once in a lifetime opportunity to restore Laurel Creek on the south edge of the project. They
are proposing a bike lane, but this is an amazing opportunity to create green space and re daylight
the creek. In addition, whether by the project sponsor or the City, consideration should be given to
flood mitigation as large parts of the Village were flooded in 2021 by Laurel Creek.

5. Caltrain Access
While there's probably some reason for it, access to the Hillsdale Caltrain station is convoluted in that
you can't go more directly from the station through the parking lot of Michael's as a more direct

about: blank?windowld=SecondaryReadingPane17 1/2
358 of 551


--- page 359 ---
route.  Maybe the project could consider improving that connection to make access more direct.
6. Mall Play Area
The current turf area in the Hillsdale North section is great for kids, even though it lacks any play
structures.  Kids love to run around, and most of them end up climbing/jumping off the decorative
rocks which isn't very safe.  It is very nice to give your kids an area to run around after a meal while the
parents finish up.  It would be nice to have another outdoor play area near the new restaurants but
that had more play-centered structures to entertain kids.
Thank you for passing along these comments.
Jared Willis 
10/29/25, 11:42 AM

--- page 362 ---
To: City Council (San Mateo) <CityCouncil@cityofsanmateo.org>
Cc: Alex Khojikian <akhojikian@cityofsanmateo.org>; Prasanna Rasiah
<prasiah@cityofsanmateo.org>
Subject: Neighborhood Concerns Re: Hillsdale Mall Redevelopment & Beyond
 
 
All, 
 
Attached are points in no particular order that were brought up at the March
Beresford Hillsdale Neighborhood Association meeting in regard to concerns over
the Hillsdale Mall redevelopment and other redevelopments impacting San Mateo's
Highway 92 access. The Mayor and Councilmember Loraine asked for this
information, which we feel is also critical to share with the rest of you.
It should be noted that since the Mayor said there are 6 projects under application
who have submitted utilizing Senate Bill 330, this is concerning due to the following: 
Fee and Standard Freeze - 
Upon submitting a preliminary application and paying the permit processing fee,
developers can "freeze" the fees and development standards that apply to their
project. 
Preventing New Regulations -
This "freeze" prevents local governments from applying newly enacted ordinances,
policies, or standards to the project after the preliminary application was submitted. 
It has been expressed by residents that the feeling is that demands placed on
current staff are too great, and that they have neither the time nor level of expertise
that the developers likely have with their own land use attorneys.  The City is urged
to invest to protect themselves and get the most out of these projects before it is too
late.
 
Sincerely,
 
The BHNA Board

attachments, is intended only for the use of the individual or entity to which it is
addressed. It may contain information that is confidential and prohibited from disclosure.
If you are not the intended recipient, you are hereby notified that any dissemination or
copying of this message or any attachment is strictly prohibited. If you have received this
message in error, please notify the original sender immediately by telephone or by return
e mail and delete this message along with any attachments from your computer. Thank
you.

10/29/25, 11:43 AM Opposed to the proposed development of the Hillsdale Mall Property - Elaine Ling - Outlook

# Outlook

Opposed to the proposed development of the Hillsdale Mall Property

Form

Date Wed 4/30/2025 5:23 PM
To Planning Commission <PlanningCommission@cityofsanmateo.org>

Cc Elaine Ling <eling@cityofsanmateo.org>

Dear Commissioners,

| want to voice my opposition to the proposed redevelopment of the Hillsdale Mall.

| am a homeowner in The Village and the proposed development will have a direct impact on my
home from increased traffic on Hillsdale,overall congestion in the surrounding area, and

other stressors on our water and school infrastructure.

How will we support the additional students for schools both K-8 and for Hillsdale High School that |
don't feel is getting enough attention. We are already struggling with teachers and having one of the
lowest paying districts in the area.

In addition, only setting a 15% for low income housing does not help the unaffordability of San Mateo
currently. Getting into a rental or a condo/home is impossible today for my kids when
condo/townhomes are going for 1M or more.

| am particularly opposed to the 8 to 10 story office space. Even the buildings that are on the other
side of El Camino are not that high. This is a ridiculous height for San Mateo, when we do not have
many buildings of that size currently. Again going back to my traffic issue.

My second opposition is the 5-8 story multi-family that hits on the same points above about it being
too high and too much for the infrastructure that we currently have to support.

| know the argument is that it is on the rail corridor but let's be honest with ourselves and know that
only 1020% of people will use the train. And since the train station moved from being right across the
street to 2 blocks down El Camino there will be less incentive to walk to the train.

This is a money grab by the developer to pack as much as they can into this space to get the most
money out of the development and it does not matter to them the real impacts to the surrounding
areas. They throw in there will be "1991" more parking spaces but what is the calculation of 2 cars per
unit and then the retail slots needed to support the shopper and the office parking? | don't see how
that is enough space to accommodate the density they are proposing.

Lastly, what is the impact to water usage with this development? We are in multi-year drought cycles
and you want to add more pressure in the water usage required for office buildings and a massive
home development.

This needs to be scaled down significantly in my opinion based on the facts listed above.

Regards
Liz Magnee-Schalch

From: Sara Kinsey

Sent: Wednesday, October 29, 2025 1:14 PM

To: City Council (San Mateo) <CityCouncil@cityofsanmateo.org>

Subject: Pro-growth, pro-community stance on Hillsdale Mall redevelopment

Dear Mayor and Councilmembers,

| support growth in San Mateo. | am not a “not in my backyard” voice. Our city needs to evolve to
meet the needs of residents and businesses. My concern is making sure the Hillsdale Mall
redevelopment truly balances community outcomes with investor returns.

Please condition any approvals on clear, enforceable public benefits with the ability to re evaluate as
the years progress towards project start and completion. Here is what | ask you to consider and push
for:

1. Housing that matches our needs

e Deliver housing before or in lock step with any new office. Tie office certificates of occupancy to
housing delivery.

e Include meaningful on site affordability beyond state minimums. Prioritize family sized units and
teacher, nurse, and city worker eligibility.

e Step down height and massing at neighborhood edges. Protect sunlight, privacy, and wind
comfort.

2. Mobility and traffic that actually works

e Independent peer review of the traffic study. Publish assumptions and mitigation triggers.

e Fund near term fixes on El Camino, Hillsdale, and 92 on and off ramps. Add protected bike
lanes and safe crossings to Caltrain and schools.

e Areal Transportation Demand Management plan with unbundled parking, transit subsidies, bike
parking, and car lite design. Monitor and adjust targets are missed.

https://outlook.office.com/mail/AAMkAGJmODIhYZMOLTBhM2MtNDQ3MS1 tie aint deninhnheialainhlaiaierme dna aie) ae 1/2
367 of 551


--- page 368 ---
3. Community-serving retail and daily needs
Secure a grocery covenant with minimum square footage and price point appropriate to local
families.
Require a retail mix that serves everyday needs, not only luxury. Protect small businesses with
fair lease terms and phased relocation options.
4. Public benefits that are guaranteed, not “hoped for”
Use a binding Development Agreement with measurable milestones, performance bonds, and
penalties for non delivery.
Parks, trees, and publicly accessible open space with year round programming.
Childcare, youth, and senior space on site.
5. Accountability over time
Phase approvals. No next phase until the prior phase meets housing, mobility, and public
benefit targets.
Create a community advisory committee that meets quarterly and can trigger corrective actions
if metrics slip.
I want San Mateo to grow, to welcome new neighbors, to keep families here, and to remain a place we
are proud to call home. Please make sure this project earns its height and density by delivering
concrete, enforceable community benefits, not just a slide deck and promises.
Thank you for your service and for insisting on a growth plan that works for residents, small
businesses, and the next generation.
Sincerely,
Sara Kinsey
Home owner 
San Mateo, CA

Re: Hillsdale Reimagined Comment Letter - Housing Leadership Council of San Mateo
County

Dear Members of the San Mateo City Council,

The Housing Leadership Council of San Mateo County. We work with our communities and their
leaders to produce and preserve quality affordable homes.

On behalf of HLC, | would first like to commend the David D. Bohanon Organization’s continued
transparency and willingness to receive input from San Mateo residents. This approach has led
to the inclusion of 1,670 new homes, including 250 affordable homes in their Hillsdale
Reimagined proposal.

These homes are ideally located near local shops, parks and green space, and the Hayward
Park Caltrain transit station. Having both jobs and homes in transit-rich spaces like this reduces
the need for driving and allows for better employment opportunities for San Mateo community
members who rely on public transit. Less commuting leaves more time for health, family, and,
most importantly, community. A community where children have a better chance of succeeding
in school, finding a job, and building their own strong families in San Mateo.

Thank you for your continued leadership, and we urge you to support a proposal that allows for
the greatest feasible number of (especially affordable) homes.

Sincerely,

Ken Chan

From:
Rick Bonilla
To:
City Council (San Mateo); Clerk
Cc:
Alex Khojikian; Zachary Dahl
Subject:
Hillsdale Shopping Center Redevelopment proposal
Date:
Monday, November 3, 2025 10:17:58 AM
Dear San Mateo City Council, 
 The Hillsdale Reimagined proposal represents exactly the forward-looking, sustainable
development San Mateo should be embracing. It would bring much-needed new housing,
support high-quality union jobs, and place both where they belong—right next to
major transit options. 
 This project would deliver up to 1,670 new homes on a site that currently provides
none. Concentrating new homes and employment opportunities beside the Hillsdale
Caltrain Station is smart planning: it reduces traffic, strengthens public transit use,
and meaningfully lowers greenhouse-gas emissions. The development not only
meets but exceeds San Mateo’s Housing Element targets for this location, advancing
our adopted plan to place homes near transit. Importantly, it also includes affordable
housing and new public spaces that will serve the entire community. 
 Some residents have cited a supposed “five-to-one” jobs-to-housing ratio, but that
figure is misleading. The 2.1 million square feet of commercial space referenced
reflects gross building area—including parking, lobbies, and other non-office space—
and a portion of the project includes retail, not office uses. Regardless, a balanced
community benefits from having both homes and workplaces located near high-
quality transit. 
 Across the country, malls are declining as shopping patterns change. Cities that fail
to adapt are left with empty properties and lost gathering places. Moving forward with
this plan allows Hillsdale to evolve into a vibrant, mixed-use neighborhood instead of
falling into disuse. 
 Hillsdale Reimagined is exactly the type of thoughtful, future-positive investment San
Mateo needs if we are to evolve and thrive in this 21st century and beyond. 
 Sincerely,
 Rick Bonilla 
San Mateo Resident, former Council Member and Mayor

--- page 376 ---
From:
David Brownman
To:
Elaine Ling
Cc:
Clerk; City Council (San Mateo)
Subject:
Hillsdale Mall Redevelopment Feedback
Date:
Sunday, November 2, 2025 10:08:16 AM
Dear City Council,
My name is David and my wife and I have lived here in San Mateo since 2021. We're a short,
5 min walk from Hillsdale Mall and visit it often. Between shopping and restaurants, we're
there at least weekly.
I'm writing to voice my tentative support for the "Hillsdale Reimagined" project that the
council will be evaluating this week. I like how much housing their plan includes and I like
that they're prioritizing walkable space. Third spaces are the heart of a community and I think
this development, once complete, will serve that purpose well. 
I also like the idea of building more densely along transit corridors, which their plan does well.
Most importantly, their plan includes a good amount of housing, which will hopefully infuse
our expensive housing market with some much needed density. 
That said, there are a couple of design elements that I'd like to see improved. 
Firstly, there's a lot of office space in the current plan. I understand that high commercial rents
help subsidize the rest of the project. But, business districts are empty on evenings and
weekends. I worry that devoting too much square footage for offices will make our new
development feel abandoned outside the work week. It would be nice to repurpose some of the
proposed space for retail and housing. 
Secondly, I'm a little concerned with the amount of interior roads on the project. While some
roads are necessary to service the residences and businesses, pedestrian-only spaces are so
much more pleasant to use, when possible. Rather than routing roads through the
development, where mall goers will need to cross streets to access other parts of the mall, I'd
love a larger focus on  roads around the development and underground tunnels/parking where
possible. We have the opportunity to really lead the way in car-free and car-minimal spaces
and I hope we can take charge here. Plus, minimizing pedestrian crossings would support the
Vision Zero goals in the city's general plan. 
Lastly, I cannot stress the importance of Trader Joe's enough. Having a grocery store within
walking distance is a huge boon to our neighborhood and I'm worried that the developers
 haven't locked them in as a new tenant yet. They've got to do everything in their power to
make sure TJ's sticks around, minimizing closure time where possible. Its loss would be
immeasurable, especially with all the beautiful new housing they're building. Give them free
rent if that's what it takes, but make it happen. Don't be forever thought of as "the project that
killed our TJ's". 
So, that's where I'm at: supportive of the project, excited for more density and housing. But
also, concerned about office space, pedestrian safety, and Trader Joe's. 
Thanks very much for reading and please don't hesitate to reach out if I can answer any
questions or provide more feedback.
376 of 551

David Brownman

Dear Members of the San Mateo City Council, 
     As someone who grew up in San Mateo, I have wonderful memories of spending time at 
Hillsdale Mall with friends and family. From frantic last-minute holiday shopping at 
Nordstrom to building my first skateboard at Zumiez and spending lazy Sundays at the old 
food court (Hot Dog on a Stick, anyone?), I am truly grateful that this place was around 
when I was a teenager. I worry about the gradual loss of third spaces in this community, 
that is, places where people can congregate outside of work, school and home.  
     The proposed scale of this development is difficult to digest, especially when it involves 
a long-standing community benefit like the mall.  A flurry of questions arise – how will this 
impact the neighborhood feel? What about traffic on El Camino and West Hillsdale? Where 
will middle and high schoolers spend time if the mall is no longer there for them? These are 
all valid questions that deserve answers, and it is my hope that the Council will work with 
Bohannan and its partners to develop responses that work for San Mateans.  
     Taking a look at the mall today, consumers have voted with our wallets, choosing the 
convenience of online shopping over the in-person shopping experience. As a result, retail 
stores are struggling mightily, and many more storefronts at the mall stand vacant or see 
only temporary, seasonal use than in past years. At the same time, state legislators have 
passed new laws in the hopes of boosting housing development such as amendments to 
the Housing Accountability Act that create strict deadlines for cities to review and decide 
on projects. So, we find ourselves at a crossroads. Redevelopment of the mall will 
fundamentally transform a section of the city and create much-needed housing, especially 
multi-bedroom units for families. This transformation will not happen overnight, but over a 
span of around two decades, which will present its own challenges for nearby residents as 
amenities like Trader Joe’s and Barnes and Noble close over the span of construction. I ask 
the Council to work with Bohannon, which promotes itself as a “family-owned firm” that 
“develops projects that benefit [its] local communities,” to expand the amount of green and 
open community space in the project proposal and to consider reducing the sizeable office 
space footprint. Moreover, as city staff and the Council continue the iterative process of 
review and discussion with the developer over the course of this project, I urge your 
comprehensive consideration of pedestrian connectivity with Caltrain’s nearby Hillsdale 
Station and with neighboring residential neighborhoods. While I am still cautious about the 
immense scale of this proposal, I am generally optimistic that the planned housing and 
retail development will ultimately be a net positive for this city and its residents. 
Thank you, 
James Ford

This comment is in reference to the November 3 city council meeting agenda, item #13, "Hillsdale Mall
Redevelopment Application — Informational"
Currently office space vacancy rates remain high in the county, at nearly 25%.  At the same time, the
city (and indeed the entire Peninsula) struggles with meeting housing needs, especially affordable
housing needs.
Why are we considering adding one single square foot of unneeded office space to the city when
housing is so desperately needed (and mandated by the state)?
This vicious circle is all too familiar: we complain there's not enough housing, then we greenlight more
office space, and then we complain that there's not enough housing.  We also complain there's not
enough space to build the housing we need, thus we need to increase density and height, completely
disregarding the fact that the space was there, but we chose to build empty offices instead of housing
on it.
We should be looking at mixed-use development for Hillsdale, not more office space.  It's so close to
transit already, it would make a perfect location for more homes, coupled together with shops and
restaurants for those who live there and those who would visit.  Additionally it would benefit from a
few "anchors" like an affordable grocery store (such as a Safeway or Lucky), as well as green space for
residents and visitors alike.
This could be a vibrant new semi-residential neighborhood and an asset to the community instead of
soulless, empty offices which only benefit the developer.
   Nicklas Johnson

Subject: Bohannon Companies/Bohannon Development Company - A Call for 
Accountability and Justice in Housing Development 
Dear Bohannon Development Company,  
Have you once again forgotten me? Allow me to introduce myself. I am a 
descendant of legal citizens who were deliberately excluded from homeownership 
by the very policies championed by Mr. Bohanon beginning in 1938. The direct 
result of those cruel and discriminatory actions has left my family in generational 
poverty—while his descendants now enjoy wealth built on the deprivation of others. 
You stripped us of dignity based solely on the color of our skin. You now claim that 
this project will serve the community by addressing the housing crisis and 
transforming this location into the “heart of the Peninsula.” Yet when I hear the 
phrase “unparalleled quality,” I hear a dog whistle—a signal that this project is not 
truly intended for families like mine. 
You say that including more affordable housing “does not pencil out”. We 
understand all too well what that means. Our current rent of $3,200 a month forces 
us to work multiple jobs and rely on assistance just to give our children a modest 
Christmas. 
So, I am asking—no, urging—you to include a substantial number of very low-cost 
housing units in this project. Not as charity, but as a step toward acknowledging 
and repairing the historic harm your predecessors inflicted upon our community. 
It is long past time for accountability. The exclusionary policies and practices that 
you continue to perpetuate have condemned generations of people of color to 
poverty—a fate that is unjust and inhumane. Justice demands reparation, not 
rhetoric. 
Sincerely, 
Alexis Lewis

Dear San Mateo City Council Members,

redevelopment plan, in advance of tonight's meeting. As a San Mateo resident, |
believe this plan will negatively impact our community in several ways:

1. **Loss of Essential Retail Access**: The demolition of major portions of the mall
will create a retail desert in our neighborhood, forcing residents to travel long
distances for basic shopping needs.

2. ““Infrastructure Strain**: The proposed 10-story buildings will overwhelm our
existing infrastructure, including roads, utilities, and public services, without adequate
planning or mitigation.

3. **Community Impact**: Hillsdale Mall has been a community gathering place for
70 years. This plan erases our shared history and eliminates a vital social hub for
families, seniors, and young people.

4. **Alternative Solutions**: Other communities have successfully revitalized their
malls without demolition. We should explore adaptive reuse, mixed-use concepts, and
community-focused development instead.

By now, you've likely noticed the growing number of local voters speaking up on
Nextdoor and Reddit who also oppose demolishing the ONLY indoor retail shopping
mall in San Mateo in exchange for more vacant commercial spaces, high-priced
housing, strain on our aged infrastructure and potential loss of city tax revenue.

For more details on the impact to our San Mateo community, alternatives and
actions to boost visibility of this major local issue, visit the website:

| urge you to reconsider this plan and work with our community to find a solution that
preserves Hillsdale Mall while addressing the area's development needs.

Thank you for your time and consideration.

Respectfully,

Laura Roedel

Dear San Mateo City Council,
 
On Monday, you will hold the first study session regarding the proposed Hillsdale Mall
redevelopment. I want to express my full support for this project and share how excited I am
about the vision being presented. I believe it will be tremendously beneficial for San Mateo
and our community as a whole.
 
This project brings a number of clear advantages:
Over 1,600 new housing units, significantly advancing our city’s housing goals and
exceeding the targets set in the General Plan for this site.
A thoughtful site layout that respects existing single-family neighborhoods by placing
low-rise townhomes along the perimeter and concentrating taller office buildings along
El Camino Real.
Retention of the newer mall section, preserving a valuable community amenity.
Creation of a vibrant retail plaza that will serve as a welcoming gathering place for
residents and visitors alike.
Addition of modern office space, bringing new jobs and economic vitality to the area.
The site leverages excellent transit access, with immediate proximity to Caltrain and
convenient connections to Highways 101 and 92.
As a qualifying SB 330 project, it will benefit from streamlined approvals and legal
protections that ensure timely progress.
 
Since there is no legal basis to deny this project due to its housing designation, I encourage the
Council and community to focus on thoughtful implementation and long-term success. I also
appreciate the developer’s proactive communication and community engagement to date.
 
As the process moves forward, I would urge the City to ensure:
The inclusion of at least one full-service grocery store to replace Trader Joe’s and
ideally another specialized grocer to meet diverse neighborhood needs while reducing
car-dependent shopping trips of our new residents.
A robust traffic management and mitigation plan addressing both the multi-year
construction period and post-completion traffic impacts.
A comprehensive plan for multi-modal connectivity, supporting safe and efficient
walking, biking, driving, and transit access.
 
This proposal will significantly enhance the livability and attractiveness of San Mateo for the
benefit of our entire community.
 
Thank you for your leadership and for advancing a project that will meaningfully shape San
Mateo’s future for the better.
 
Best, Kolja

Highway 92, Hillsdale Boulevard, and nearby neighborhood streets. Can our schools, water,

city revenue?

timeline and how will disruption be minimized? How will the city prevent office space from

Dear San Mateo City Council,

I was born and raised in the Bay Area, and my husband and I feel incredibly fortunate that in
2022 we were able to buy a home here in San Mateo, near Hillsdale, just half an hour from
where I grew up. Still, it was an enormous stretch financially, and it’s clear to me that housing
costs have gotten out of hand in the Bay Area. If we want our children to have the opportunity
to live in the communities where they grew up, we need to build more housing.

That’s why I fully support the Hillsdale Reimagined project. I’ve read through the plans
posted in October in detail, and I’m very excited for this new vision for Hillsdale. The North
Block has been a wonderful success, feels vibrant and welcoming, and is always very active.
The South Block, by contrast, is starting to become an eyesore. I believe the proposed plan
strikes the right balance between adding needed housing, updating retail, and creating new
community spaces.

I’m especially excited about the new Hillsdale Square and the opportunity for community
programming there like concerts, art, or family events. One small detail I’d like to advocate
for is the use of real grass rather than artificial turf in Hillsdale Square and the triangle park
near the Edison roundabout. Real grass will make these parks more enjoyable to sit on and
better for the environment by reducing plastic runoff and improving water absorption in the
watershed.

I’m also excited to see that the plan includes a new grocery store at 31st and El Camino. I
think the development would also benefit from including a smaller grocery or convenience
store space in addition. Something more suited for a Trader Joe’s or similar neighborhood
market.

My biggest concern is the project timeline. I worry that this incredible opportunity might be
delayed for years. I urge the City Council to do everything possible to move the project

forward quickly so the community can benefit sooner.

Thank you for your time and for supporting a project that brings new housing, open space, and
revitalized retail to San Mateo.

Sincerely,
Alexandra Westbrook

From: 
 
Sent: Monday, November 3, 2025 5:36 PM
To: City Council (San Mateo) <CityCouncil@cityofsanmateo.org>
Subject: Hillsdale Reimagined - comments
 
Dear City Council,     I’ve lived in San Mateo for over 20 years, and I’m writing to share my
support for the Hillsdale Reimagined project. Our city is facing a housing shortage, and
this project could help to address a significant need.
 
Building up to 1,670 new homes—both for sale and for rent—would be outstanding and
exactly what San Mateo needs. This project gives families and young people more
options to stay and grow in our community – yay.
 
As part of this, building up to 250 affordable homes is a big deal. Teachers, nurses, and
service workers—people who make our city run—should be able to live here too. Right
now, that’s  pretty difficult. This project adds a mix of homes for all kinds of people, not
just those who can afford to buy a house.
 
It’s great to build housing near Caltrain and on land that’s already developed. Plus, the
plan includes shops and open spaces -  will make it a comfortable neighborhood.
 
I hope you’ll support Hillsdale Reimagined.  It’s the kind of project that shows San Mateo
cares about housing for all the people of this wonderful city.
 
Best regards and thank you,   
 
    Susan Ketcham

Good afternoon, City Council Members,

My name is Rubi Salazar, and I am a resident of San Mateo, where I work at the Youth
Leadership Institute, serving programs in San Mateo County. I urge the council to consider the
impacts of this project, especially on the current community of San Mateo. If there is to be
development for housing, I ask that 75%, if not all, be dedicated to affordable housing. As a
community member and adult ally to youth, affordable housing is a major issue. Many of our
youth want to continue living in San Mateo County after graduation, but given the cost of
living, it is nearly impossible unless youth have family already in the area.

Please take this into consideration when learning more about this project.

From: Robert Wick 
 
Sent: Monday, November 3, 2025 6:17 PM
To: City Council (San Mateo) <CityCouncil@cityofsanmateo.org>
Subject: Hillsdale Mall, CSM, Campus Drive, Clearview (Tesla/Go Pro Site)Developments
 
Greetings,
 
November 3, 2025
 
I am very concerned regarding the current lack of planned infrastructure for planned
development in San Mateo. There seems to be a lack of restriction due to major
oversights by developers proposed plans. The concerns I have are purely objective:
 
1. San Mateo does not have schools that would support the student population
increase. There are currently waiting lists for all High, Middle and Elementary Schools
throughout San Mateo County. (San Mateo, Aragon, Hillsdale are at capacity)
Elementary Schools (Baywood and Highlands) over capacity currently and no space
available to build out. 
 
Where will the new schools be located for all the new residents/students? How will they
be financed/budgeted? 
 
2. Traffic is at a standstill along the 92  and 101 corridors with additional traffic trying to
circumvent the congestion through city streets during peak hours. Anyone living near
West Hilldale will attest that additional congestion will result from new compliance
regulations from new developments. The impact of Harvest Developments on Campus
Drive (290 Residential Units) and proposed development at the site of Tesla Go pro site
418 of 551

--- page 419 ---
on Clearview Way (222 units). CSM Dormitories proposed (316 Units). 
 
The Traffic Study performed for Harvest Properties was done during the peak of
COVID Lockdown which INVALIDATES the study. The study needs to be done with
current traffic conditions and the council should require it.
 
3. Parking - Each new home condo adds at least 2 and up to 4 vehicles to each
residence. Developers do not appear to consider this factor but appear to maximize
home sale and minimize amenities such as parking spaces for each home and visitors in
high density housing.  
 
Fantasy Commutership by developers - Traffic studies for developers use the bus, train
and bikes and other means as a percentage of commuters in studies when actual
alternative ridership for San Mateo do not support the assertion. San Mateo residents
use cars and trains for transportation. Some kids use the bus but parents drive their kids
to school every day.
 
I am not anti-development for San Mateo but I do expect the city council to provide
leadership for it to be done responsibly with limits to scope and size. There should be
additional input from an area greater than 1000 ft from a proposed development project
(who drafted that city or state requirement). This should be changed as citizenry being
greatly affected must be allowed a voice for input for development.
 
SB 330 and the recent passing of height limits in San Mateo for the purpose of compliance
with state limits seems to have opened Pandora's box.
 
The Council still retains the ability to limit scope and height of proposed projects during
proposal reviews. Objective concerns should be part of the Council's remedies for
proposed projects in order to assure the quality of living in San Mateo remains at a high
standard. 
 
Robert Wick
VP Clearview Townhome HOA

HOUSING ACCOUNTABILITY ACT LIMITS CITY DISCRETION
HOUSING ACCOUNTABILITY ACT LIMITS CITY DISCRETION
• Applies to “housing development projects” – revised 1/1/25
• HCD:  Hillsdale development qualifies under new definition 
•
50 percent of the net new residential use and >500 Units, etc.
• City can require compliance with objective, quantifiable, 
written standards (e.g., in the General Plan/ Zoning Code)
• City cannot deny or reduce project density absent a specific 
adverse impact on public health or safety: 
• a significant, quantifiable, direct, and unavoidable impact, based 
on objective, identified written health or safety standards

CITY OF SAN MATEO
CITY OF SAN MATEO
Agenda Report
City Hall
330 W. 20th Avenue
San Mateo CA 94403
www.cityofsanmateo.org
Agenda Number:  14
Section Name: NEW BUSINESS
Account Number: 10-1311
File ID: 25-10587
TO:
City Council
FROM:
Alex Khojikian, City Manager
PREPARED BY:
City Manager's Office
MEETING DATE:
November 3, 2025 
SUBJECT:
Economic Development Work Plan 2026-2029 — Adoption
RECOMMENDATION:
Adopt Economic Development Work Plan 2026–2029. 
BACKGROUND:
On November 4, 2024, the City Council approved a framework for a three-year Economic Development Work Plan, to 
guide implementation, foster partnerships and strengthen and diversify the City’s economic base. The Work Plan affirms 
the City’s commitment to building a more welcoming, adaptable, inclusive, and resilient business and visitor community. A 
thriving local economy is essential to community vibrancy and long-term fiscal stability. Following Council direction to 
proceed with developing this Work Plan, the City adopted its 2025 Strategic Plan, which identifies Economic Development 
as one of the ten key Initiatives to focus on over the next five years.
The proposed 2026-2029 Economic Development Workplan (Attachment 1) builds on the Council approved framework and 
is designed to be flexible, data-driven, and responsive to changing market conditions.
The proposed Economic Development Work Plan is informed by: 
•
Analysis of San Mateo’s economic strengths, weaknesses, opportunities, and threats (SWOT);
•
Alignment with General Plan 2040 goals related to economic development;
•
Engagement with stakeholders including the San Mateo Area Chamber of Commerce, Downtown San Mateo 
Association (DSMA), developers, business owners, and residents;
•
A collaborative cross-departmental staff review; and
•
The City’s Strategic Plan.
PURPOSE
The Economic Development Work Plan provides a clear framework to guide staff in advancing San Mateo’s economic 
priorities by building on the City’s strengths, expanding opportunities, and addressing emerging challenges. Through 
partnerships with the Downtown San Mateo Association, Chamber of Commerce, SAMCEDA, The SF Peninsula, workforce 
organizations, and neighborhood-serving business districts, the Plan supports long-term economic sustainability and 
community vibrancy.
FINDINGS
Following Council’s approval of the initial economic development framework, staff conducted extensive outreach with 
neighborhood businesses, the Chamber of Commerce, Downtown San Mateo Association, commercial brokers, community 
representatives, and internal cross-departmental staff to identify San Mateo’s key strengths, weaknesses, opportunities 
512 of 551

--- page 513 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
and threats (SWOT). The following is a summary of the key findings:
•
Strength - Vibrant Downtown and Active Business Districts – The City has a walkable urban core and a diverse mix 
of businesses and community amenities that attract residents and visitors.
•
Strength - Strategic Central Location and Strong Transit Access – The City’s mid-peninsula location, three Caltrain 
stations, SamTrans connections, and highways 101/280/92 access support business activity and workforce 
mobility.
•
Strength - Diverse and Dynamic Business Base – The City’s innovative and inclusive business environment 
supports entrepreneurship and small business growth and is reinforced by a culturally diverse and safe 
community.
•
Weakness - High Costs and Limited Space Constrain Growth – The City’s high operating and real estate costs, loss 
of neighborhood-serving grocers, and limited sites for development create barriers to business expansion.
•
Weakness - Perception of Complex Permitting and Regulations – Feedback from businesses and development 
stakeholders indicates that the City’s permitting processes can be perceived as complex, highlighting the need for 
continued streamlining and improved transparency.
•
Weakness - Declining Hotel Inventory Limits Economic Potential – The reduction in hotel properties limits the 
City’s ability to capture visitor spending, business travel demand, and tourism-related economic activity.
•
Opportunities - Development and Market Momentum –  Recent land use policy changes, including the passage of 
Measure T and SB 330 and the launch of the Downtown Specific Plan in 2026, create opportunities for the City to 
support mixed-use development, business attraction and retention, and new investment.
•
Threats - External Economic Pressures and Regional Competition –  Regional housing affordability challenges, 
shifting retail trends, broader economic uncertainty, and increased competition from neighboring cities may 
impact the City’s ability to retain businesses, sustain workforce, and support long-term economic vitality.
STRATEGIC GOALS AND WORK PROGRAM
Staff utilized Council direction, the results of the SWOT analysis, and stakeholder input to develop the following strategic 
goals and work program. Full descriptions are provided in the 2026–2029 Economic Development Work Plan (Attachment 
1).
1.
Expand and diversify tax revenue sources: Attraction, expansion, and retention of businesses across sectors 
2.
Foster a business-friendly environment: Reduce barriers, streamline permitting, and improve customer service
3.
Support Workforce Development and Employment Training Opportunities: Support job creation at all levels and 
strengthen pathways for economic mobility
4.
Increase workforce and affordable housing access: partner on housing and transportation efforts
5.
Enhance entertainment and cultural offerings: Strengthen commercial districts and diversify amenities 
6.
Maintain safe vibrant business districts: Strengthen public safety partnerships, crime prevention programs, 
placemaking activities, and marketing
7.
Align with General Plan 2040 and Downtown Specific Plan: Integrate with City planning priorities 
PARTNERSHIP ALIGNMENT
The Economic Development Work Plan also prioritizes collaboration with key community partners to maximize impact and 
leverage resources, including the San Mateo Area Chamber of Commerce, Downtown San Mateo Association (DSMA), San 
Mateo County Economic Development Association (SAMCEDA), The San Francisco Peninsula Tourism Group, NOVAworks, 
JobTrain, Job Seeker Center, and the College of San Mateo.
EVALUATION AND IMPLEMENTATION
The Economic Development Work Plan will be evaluated annually to ensure accountability and responsiveness to changing 
economic conditions. Progress will be measured through key indicators such as vacancy rates, sales tax revenue, 
unemployment trends, and broker inquiries. Implementation will be coordinated across City departments and with 
external partners such as the Chamber of Commerce and the Downtown San Mateo Association (DSMA). Economic 
Development staff will provide quarterly updates through the City Council’s weekly reports, an annual progress report, and 
a Year 3 review.
513 of 551

--- page 514 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
The proposed Economic Development Work Plan provides a clear path for implementation and continuous improvement. 
Staff recommends approval of the proposed Economic Development Work Plan.
BUDGET IMPACT:
There is no budget impact for the proposed Economic Development Work Plan and framework. No appropriation is 
required at this time. 
ENVIRONMENTAL DETERMINATION:
This item is not a project subject to CEQA, because it is an organizational or administrative activity that will not result in 
direct or indirect physical changes in the environment. (CEQA Guidelines Section 15378(b)(5).)
NOTICE PROVIDED
All meeting noticing requirements were met.
ATTACHMENTS

Att 3 – Appendix B: Current and Planned Economic Development Initiatives

EXECUTIVE SUMMARY 
In 2024, the City Council adopted a framework for a three-year Economic Development 
Work Plan to guide implementation, foster partnerships, and strengthen and diversify 
the City’s economic base. Building on this direction, the 2026–2029 Work Plan is 
designed to be flexible, data-driven, and responsive to changing market conditions. 
Developed in alignment with the City’s 2025 Strategic Plan and General Plan 2040, the 
Work Plan is grounded in stakeholder engagement, analysis of San Mateo’s economic 
strengths, weaknesses, opportunities, and threats, and cross-departmental review. The 
Work Plan will help staff advance the City’s economic priorities by building on existing 
strengths, expanding opportunities, and addressing emerging challenges. To ensure 
accountability and transparency, it incorporates annual performance reviews with key 
indicators such as vacancy rates and sales tax trends, along with regular progress 
updates to the City Council.
By focusing on economic resilience, diversification, and strong partnerships, the Work 
Plan advances the City’s commitment to a welcoming, adaptable, and inclusive 
economy that benefits residents, businesses, and visitors alike.
516 of 551

--- page 517 ---
~ 1 ~
517 of 551

--- page 518 ---
~ 2 ~
I. INTRODUCTION
San Mateo is an evolving city navigating changing economic conditions that present 
fiscal challenges, but also create opportunities for innovation, adaptation, and 
diversification of the tax base to ensure long-term resilience. With major development 
on the horizon and evolving regional economic trends, the City Council directed staff to 
prepare an Economic Development Work Plan. The Work Plan is designed to be 
adaptable and action-oriented, focusing on diversifying the tax base, strengthening 
economic vitality, retaining local businesses, and attracting new investment to ensure 
long-term economic stability.
The 2026–2029 Economic Development Work Plan builds on:
•
An analysis of San Mateo’s economic strengths, weaknesses, opportunities, and 
threats
•
General Plan 2040 Goals related to Economic Development
•
Extensive stakeholder engagement with the San Mateo Area Chamber of 
Commerce, Downtown San Mateo Association, Commercial real estate brokers, 
developers, business owners, and residents
•
The City’s Strategic Plan 
518 of 551

--- page 519 ---
~ 2 ~
519 of 551

--- page 520 ---
~ 3 ~
II. SWOT SUMMARY 
After the City Council approved the initial Economic Development framework in 
November 2024, staff conducted extensive outreach with neighborhood business 
groups, brokers, community members, developers, and City departments to gather and 
synthesize input, which informed a summary of San Mateo’s economic strengths and 
weaknesses.
Strengths
❖Vitality - Walkable, vibrant Downtown with diverse 
restaurants and services
❖Connected - Three Caltrain stations – unique on the 
Peninsula
❖Amenities-Filled - Central Park and major shopping hubs 
(Hillsdale, Downtown, Bridgepointe, 25th Avenue)
❖Innovative - Dynamic startup and corporate ecosystem (AI, 
gaming, finance, biotech incubator, tech incubators)
❖Inclusive Community - Strong civic engagement, cultural 
diversity, and high quality of life
❖Strategically located in the region with close proximity to 
San Mateo Bridge
❖Strong restaurant presence  
❖Safe community
❖Parking– good parking access, with multiple public garages 
Downtown
❖Vibrant business nodes
Weaknesses
❖Hospitality - reduced hotel inventory, including loss of 
Marriot

III. STRATEGIC GOALS 
These strategic goals provide a framework to guide decision-making, focus resources, 
and achieve measurable progress in economic development.
  
  1. Expand and Diversify Tax Revenue Sources 
     (Attraction, expansion, and retention of businesses across sectors)
  
 2. Foster a Business-Friendly Environment  
    (Reduce barriers, streamline permitting, and improve customer service)
   3. Support Workforce Development and Employment Training
      Opportunties 
   (Support job creation at all levels, strengthen pathways for economic mobility)
  4. Increase Workforce and Affordable Housing Access
     (Partner on housing and transportation efforts)
  5. Enhance Entertainment and Cultural Offerings
       (Strengthen commercial districts and diversify amenities) 
  6. Maintain Safe, Vibrant Business Districts 
    (Public safety partnerships, crime prevention programs, placemaking activities, 
and marketing) 
 7. Align with General Plan 2040 & Downtown Specific 
Plan
     (Intergrate with City's Planning priorities)

IV. STRATEGIC GOALS & WORK PROGRAM
Guided by City Council direction and stakeholder input, the following detailed work 
program carries out the City’s seven strategic goals to foster a vibrant and resilient 
economy.
1.
Expand and Diversify Tax Revenue Sources
Key actions will involve working with partners to:
•
Diversify commercial occupancies by hosting quarterly broker meetings to 
identify vacancies, subleases, and available properties
•
Grow and attract growth-stage businesses that generate consistent revenue and 
high-quality jobs to strengthen the City’s economic base and align with the City’s 
2025 Strategic Plan and General Plan 2040
•
Identify and pursue new revenue streams by exploring billboard policies, 
conducting economic assessments of City-owned properties, evaluating a 
business improvement district (BID) or property improvement district (PBID), and 
explore opportunities to increase general fund revenue
•
Leverage the existing incubator ecosystem by collaborating with San Mateo’s 
private incubators, venture capital firms to amplify resources for startups in AI, 
fintech, biosciences, software development, and direct investment
•
Monitor regional market trends to inform future land use and redevelopment 
strategies
•
Strengthen economic intelligence by tracking and analyzing economic 
development data, trends, and market activity
2. Foster A Business-Friendly Environment
Key actions will involve working with partners to:
•
Increase social media engagement to amplify economic development 
messaging, strengthen community connections, and increase visibility
•
Expand support for minority-owned and small businesses by partnering with the 
San Mateo Area Chamber of Commerce, Downtown San Mateo Association, 
525 of 551

--- page 526 ---
~ 7 ~
Small Business Development Center, Renaissance Entrepreneurship Center, and 
other organizations that support minority-owned and small businesses 
•
Facilitate business expansion and investment by coordinating meetings between 
City staff and businesses pursuing development, attraction, or growth plans, while 
expanding express permitting options and streamlining tenant improvement 
permits
•
Promote business diversity by marketing San Mateo as a welcoming environment 
for businesses of all sizes and sectors
•
Promote and leverage federal and state programs, grants, and tax incentives to 
support business growth and investment
•
Recognize nonprofits, community partners, and intergovernmental service 
agencies and support their growth and presence 
•
Strengthen business retention and growth through proactive outreach to high-
revenue and high-employment companies (e.g., Sony, Roblox, Zynga, Franklin 
Templeton) to understand their needs and provide support for expansion and 
retention
•
Update the City’s Economic Development webpage to better connect business 
resources
3.
Support Workforce Development and Promote Training 
Opportunities
Key actions will involve working with partners to:
•     Expand workforce training opportunities by collaborating with NOVAWorks, 
JobTrain, the Main Library’s Job Seeker’s Center, Worker’s Resource Center, 
and College of San Mateo
•    Coordinate programs to ensure students and displaced workers gain current, in-
demand skills by advising workforce boards on businesses employment needs
•
Leverage state and federal tax incentives to expand training and employment 
programs for businesses
•
Promote youth employment by connecting internships, apprenticeships, and 
career exploration programs with schools and employers
•
Support a diverse local economy by working with unions, trade organizations, 
and industry partners to expand access to high-quality jobs and career pathways

Strengthen the local economy by improving last-mile connections, reducing

Support “shop local” initiatives and special events in partnership with the DSMA 
and Chamber, parks and recreation, and other organization interested in using 
events to promote San Mateo

Activate underutilized spaces with pop-ups, outdoor markets, and farmers

•
Collaborate with Police on the Business Watch program and their Real Time 
Information Center to provide a welcoming public safety presence in business 
districts

Overview of the Current Economic Landscape
San Mateo’s economy is anchored by a diverse mix of industries, high household 
incomes, and a strong retail and culinary scene. Its central Peninsula location and 
unmatched connectivity to regional markets provide a competitive advantage. However, 
pressures from online retail, rising costs, and the shift to remote work require adaptive 
strategies to sustain long-term competitiveness.
The City spans approximately 11 square miles (excluding water areas) and features 
four prominent retail districts: Downtown, Hillsdale Shopping Center, Bay Meadows, and 
Bridgepointe Shopping Center.
Downtown San Mateo
Adjacent to Central Park, Downtown offers a vibrant mix of shops, 
restaurants, offices, housing, and a Century 12 movie theater. Well-known for 
its wide variety of global cuisines, Downtown is a pedestrian-friendly 
destination located along the Caltrain line and serves as a lively cultural and 
commercial center for the community and peninsula.
Hillsdale Shopping Center
A major regional mall featuring more than 100 stores, including anchors such 
as Trader Joe’s, Barnes & Noble, Macy’s, Nordstrom, and others, along with 
a diverse range of dining options. Conveniently accessible via Caltrain, 
Hillsdale is a key hub of retail activity on the Peninsula. Its latest expansion, 
North Block, adds additional shops like West Elm, Cinepolis, restaurants, and 
provides public gathering spaces for events and entertainment.
Bay Meadows
A modern, transit-oriented neighborhood blending residential, office, retail, 
and open space. Bay Meadows features restaurants, cafes, and services 
designed to serve both residents and visitors, with a strong emphasis on 
walkability, sustainability, and access to Caltrain. It is directly across Hillsdale 
Shopping Center. Major office users are Roblox, Survey Monkey, and 
Guidewire. 
Bridgepointe Shopping Center
A major retail destination offering a wide selection of national retailers, 
specialty stores, and dining options. Its location near Highway 92 provides 
convenient regional access, attracting shoppers from Foster City, Halfmoon 
and East Bay. 
531 of 551

--- page 532 ---
In addition to its four primary retail districts, San Mateo offers a variety of neighborhood 
shopping centers and retail nodes—such as 25th Avenue, Laurie Meadows, Laurelwood 
Shopping Center, Shoreview Shopping Center, Bel Mateo Plaza, Crystal Springs 
Village, 17th Avenue, and clusters along El Camino Real—that primarily serve the daily 
needs of surrounding neighborhoods.
Beyond retail, San Mateo has a diverse employment base anchored by fintech, gaming, 
healthcare, life sciences, and startups. Major employers—including Sony Interactive, 
Sutter Health, Kaiser, Franklin Templeton, and Roblox—contribute stability, innovation, 
and regional visibility. Retaining these industries is critical to sustaining jobs, tax 
revenues, and long-term economic resilience. Looking forward, San Mateo is also well-
positioned to attract complementary sectors such as AI/data services, expanded life 
sciences, and cleantech, which align with broader Bay Area strengths and support 
future growth.
San Mateo’s competitive advantages include its walkable urban fabric, vibrant 
downtown, unique transit access with three Caltrain stations, strong civic engagement, 
and high-performing schools. At the same time, the City faces challenges common 
across the Bay Area: high operating costs, a perception of overregulation, and ongoing 
housing affordability pressures. Still, there are opportunities to diversify retail offerings, 
recruit innovative growth-stage companies, and expand cultural programming with 
support from the Downtown Association and Chamber.
San Mateo is also dealing with bigger trends that threaten local businesses, like more 
people shopping online instead of in stores, the long-term shift to remote work and 
smaller office needs, uncertainty around interest rates, and stronger competition from 
nearby cities and global markets. Finally, the ups and downs of the national and world 
economy make planning harder, showing why flexible and resilient strategies are so 
important.
532 of 551

November 03, 2025 
SUBJECT:
City Council Priority List Fiscal Year 2025-26 — Progress Report
RECOMMENDATION:
Receive a progress report on the City Council Priority List for Fiscal Year 2025-26 and provide any direction to staff if any 
edits are desired. 
BACKGROUND:
The City Council goal-setting process includes a three-step deliberation resulting in adoption of a City Council Priority List, 
which includes staff’s proposed work plan for each initiative for the upcoming fiscal year. The first step of the process was 
the City Council Blue Sky Workshop on February 22, 2025. At the workshop, the City Council reviewed the adopted Fiscal 
Year 2024-25 Priorities, removed completed and operationalized items, reorganized the projected completion timeline for 
existing priorities, and proposed new priorities to add to the list for Fiscal Year 2025-26. On May 5, 2025, City Council 
reviewed and formally adopted the Fiscal Year 2025-26 City Council Priority List. Fiscal Year 2025-26 started on July 1, 
2025, and ends on June 30, 2026. This report focuses on the progress made from July 1 through October 10, 2025, and 
estimated progress through the end of the fiscal year. 
Staff has made progress on each of the one-year priorities and many of the longer-term priorities or has identified next 
steps for advancing these initiatives. The following are some examples:
•
Priority #7 is completed – The Council held a study session and provided policy direction on limiting overnight RV 
parking, which is now being implemented by the Police Department. 
•
Priority #11 is completed – The City Council Code of Conduct was updated. 
•
Priority #12 is completed – the Council held a study session on mobile vending and provided direction to staff, 
which is now being implemented. 
•
Priority #1 is completed – The Council adopted a Resolution documenting its agreed upon pathway toward a 
Sustainable Building Strategy. 
•
Priority #34 – Staff has made presentations to City Council on the Bellevue and Villa Terrace at-grade crossings, 
received direction for closures and phased implementation, and is now working on design and funding. 
•
Priority #17 – Construction commenced on structural repairs at the Marina Library in August 2025, with 
completion expected toward the end of 2026.  
Fiscal Year 2025-2026 will be the last year with priorities in the 1-Year, 2 to 5 Year, and 5 Years or More categories. In 
September, the City Council approved its first Citywide Strategic Plan with implementation set to begin on July 1, 2026, 
Fiscal Year 2026/27. With this new framework in place, the Council will be adopting SMART (Specific, Measurable, 
Achievable, Relevant, and Timebound) Activities instead of general priorities at the beginning of each year. Each Activity 
will fall into the category of one of the Council’s Strategic Initiatives, which were developed and agreed upon by Council 
535 of 551

--- page 536 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
and are contained in the Plan. As a result, some of the existing priorities that remain incomplete and are fit for conversion 
into SMART Activities will be proposed for conversion, while some of the broader and longer-term priorities are absorbed 
into the Initiatives as well as the Goals identified throughout the Plan. The methods for reporting on progress is outlined in 
the new Strategic Plan.  
Staff recommends City Council review the existing Fiscal Year 2025-26 Priorities and the respective updates and provide 
direction to staff if any revisions are desired. 
BUDGET IMPACT:
There is no budget impact from this agenda item. 
ENVIRONMENTAL DETERMINATION:
This information update is not a project subject to CEQA, because it is an organizational or administrative activity that will 
not result in direct or indirect physical changes in the environment. (CEQA Guidelines Section 15378(b)(5).)
